<div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_94754599" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/94754599" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen" title="Stephen"><img alt="Stephen" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1311616811p2/1413439.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/94754599?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Sep 05, 2010</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen" title="Stephen">Stephen</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=6-star-books">6-star-books</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=sherlockian">sherlockian</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=1980-1989">1980-1989</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=love-those-words">love-those-words</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=books-about-books">books-about-books</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=classics-european">classics-european</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer94754599" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer14760085595734431566">6.0 stars. On my list of "All Time Favorite" novels. This incredible book is the newest entry onto my list of favorite novels of all time. Reading this book was a one of a kind literary experience that I highly recommend everyone experience. The basic plot of the novel is an excellent murder mystery set in an Italian monastery during the 14th century and featuring an excellent "Sherlock Holmes" type character named William of Baskerville. As good as the basic plot is, the real essence of the sto</span>
<span id="freeText14760085595734431566" style="display:none">6.0 stars. On my list of "All Time Favorite" novels. This incredible book is the newest entry onto my list of favorite novels of all time. Reading this book was a one of a kind literary experience that I highly recommend everyone experience. The basic plot of the novel is an excellent murder mystery set in an Italian monastery during the 14th century and featuring an excellent "Sherlock Holmes" type character named William of Baskerville. As good as the basic plot is, the real essence of the story is a exposition on the nature of philosophies, beliefs and the ability of man to obtain knowledge of universal truths. <br><br>Apart from the above general description, this is a really tough book to describe in detail because there is so much going on throughout the narrative. I will simply end by saying that it is superbly written, highly detailed, never boring and at 600 pages I wish it was longer as I was not ready for the book to end. Definitely one that I will re-read several times. HIGHEST POSSIBLE RECOMMENDATION!!!!! </br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="14760085595734431566" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/39509947?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Dec 07, 2008</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/350677-laura" title="Laura">Laura</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350677-laura?shelf=history">history</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350677-laura?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350677-laura?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>

<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer39509947" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer12349943153966923031">This book is both astonishingly difficult and extremely rewarding. I had six years of Latin in middle and high school and have taken a course on medieval philosophy and I <em>still</em> found this one both challenging and satisfying. Anyone willing to put the work in is going to adore this one.<br>WARNING, however: this is not a trashy medieval-conspiracy novel. This is not a thriller. It is an excellent book and a perfect mystery, but it is still set in the 14th century and all of the characters are still m</br></span>
<span id="freeText12349943153966923031" style="display:none">This book is both astonishingly difficult and extremely rewarding. I had six years of Latin in middle and high school and have taken a course on medieval philosophy and I <em>still</em> found this one both challenging and satisfying. Anyone willing to put the work in is going to adore this one.<br>WARNING, however: this is not a trashy medieval-conspiracy novel. This is not a thriller. It is an excellent book and a perfect mystery, but it is still set in the 14th century and all of the characters are still monks. Also, I believe that the fact that I'm a Catholic also contributed significantly to my understanding of this novel-- it some ways it's essentially a treatise on monastic philosophy and the differences between different monastic orders. So don't read this if you want something like the Da Vinci Code. That's trash. This isn't.</br></span>
<a data-text-id="12349943153966923031" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_10076146" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10076146" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/666003-silvana" title="Silvana"><img alt="Silvana" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1347249551p2/666003.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/10076146?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Dec 06, 2007</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/666003-silvana" title="Silvana">Silvana</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>
<div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
<span class="uitext reviewText">everyone</span></div>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/666003-silvana?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/666003-silvana?shelf=bookmooch">bookmooch</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/666003-silvana?shelf=donated">donated</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/666003-silvana?shelf=used-to-own">used-to-own</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer10076146" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer5642003408888661525">What a mesmerizing (yet sometimes confusing) book. Five hundred pages, not including the introduction and post-script.<br><br>It is basically consisted of two main plots. First was the mysterious murders of monks in an Italian Franciscan abbey on the 14th century, in which a former Inquisitor named William of Baskerville and his novice turned detectives to solve the murders. Second was the so-called historic meeting between Franciscan leaders (favored by the Roman Emperor) and their archenemy, represent</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText5642003408888661525" style="display:none">What a mesmerizing (yet sometimes confusing) book. Five hundred pages, not including the introduction and post-script.<br><br>It is basically consisted of two main plots. First was the mysterious murders of monks in an Italian Franciscan abbey on the 14th century, in which a former Inquisitor named William of Baskerville and his novice turned detectives to solve the murders. Second was the so-called historic meeting between Franciscan leaders (favored by the Roman Emperor) and their archenemy, representatives of the Pope John who resided then in Avignon. Organizing the meeting was actually the main duty of William.<br><br>I have to admit that my interest lies deeper in the first plot. It's just more simple. The murders were somewhat arranged in certain fashions, mimicking the prophesied events during the sounding of seven trumpets in the Apocalypse. Plus, the Abbey itself, with all its rich legacies and precious relics, holds many secrets and intrigues; with the center of attention was its amazing library that no one can enter except the librarian. The library was said to be the most complete library in Europe, contained not only teachings on the Scriptures but also those of heretics, magicians, alchemists, etc.<br><br>William of Baskerville, our monk-detective, presented a unique character with his use of logic and modern tools. His novice, Adso of Melk, well, he's your typical boyscout, but suffering from a number of delusions and inner battles (the curse of youth, perhaps). They both embarked on a fascinating adventure in the abbey, trying to solve the mystery of the library and its almost innumerable labyrinths, deciphering symbols, facing bizarre monks ranging from hostile, mysterious, frightened, excessively curious and most of all, they all have motives for killing. Most of the victims were connected to the library. Suffice to say, the murders is caused by something in the library, which should not be seen by others.<br><br>The second plot? Well, couldn't say I really understand the narratives and dialogues, since they mostly involved theological debates whether monks were allowed to be poor. The Pope said the Franciscans were heretics while the accused said the Pope was corrupt. My brain is just not strong enough for that kind of thing. I love the quarreling part though, when the monks lost their temper, because it's so damn funny.<br><br>Despite some desperate efforts from my part to skip some pages of this book due to my lack of understanding, I still say this book has its own grandeur. Perhaps because if there's an existing library as magnificent as the one in the abbey, I shall be very interested to have to a visit there. Seduction does not always come from human flesh or splendid treasures, but also in knowledge. And sometimes it's even more dangerous.<br><br>Will I read other Eco's works? Yeah sure, why not. Next would be the Foucault's Pendulum.</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="5642003408888661525" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="section">
<div class="review" id="review_435886426" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/435886426" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6313408-jonathan" title="Jonathan"><img alt="Jonathan" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1414615395p2/6313408.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/435886426?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Feb 21, 2013</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/6313408-jonathan" title="Jonathan">Jonathan</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/611484.The_Name_of_the_Rose" title="Paperback 9780099466031">review of another edition</a>
<div>
<span class="greyText">Recommended to Jonathan by: </span>
<a href="/user/show/6431467-s-penkevich">s.penkevich</a>
</div>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=books-with-films-2">books-with-films-2</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=faith">faith</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=popularity-contest-2">popularity-contest-2</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=1001-books-to-read-before-you-die">1001-books-to-read-before-you-die</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer435886426" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer4773809990195945295"><br><strong>
<em>The Name of the Rose: A convoluted and thorny plant of beauty</em>
</strong><br><br><em>The Name of the Rose</em> ranks among some of the most complex books read by myself. However where works like <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15997.Paradise_Lost" title="Paradise Lost by John Milton">Paradise Lost</a> or <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/39063.Titus_Groan" title="Titus Groan by Mervyn Peake">Titus Groan</a> contain their complexity with the power of beautiful flowing prose this novel works at maintaining complexity through its sheer psychological and philosophical depth. Added to which the reader can observe that this sophisticated work of fiction is so tightly wound as to form a textual labyrinth like the</br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText4773809990195945295" style="display:none"><br><strong>
<em>The Name of the Rose: A convoluted and thorny plant of beauty</em>
</strong><br><br><em>The Name of the Rose</em> ranks among some of the most complex books read by myself. However where works like <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15997.Paradise_Lost" title="Paradise Lost by John Milton">Paradise Lost</a> or <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/39063.Titus_Groan" title="Titus Groan by Mervyn Peake">Titus Groan</a> contain their complexity with the power of beautiful flowing prose this novel works at maintaining complexity through its sheer psychological and philosophical depth. Added to which the reader can observe that this sophisticated work of fiction is so tightly wound as to form a textual labyrinth like the one written about within this novel.<br><br>Umberto Eco’s work may appear to be a diabolically devised mystery set in the early 13th century, yet it is so much more than this. On the surface, it contains an ideology and idea about exploring the reality of man and the human condition. Deeper down the ideology subtly implies that reality is what each individual makes of it and further, that the world consists of signs linked into other signs, much as all novels arguably link to each other. <br><br>Part of the brilliance of this text is how Eco links together the past late-medieval setting and views with modern ideas and preconceptions, providing a fascinating discussion as regards religion, particularly Catholicism. Yet, at the same time, there is a quandary posed by the text for the astute reader. Eco appears as both critic and proponent of religion within this novel. In one moment he argues that an overt attachment to God cannot help but create a flawed worldview. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Indicated in sequences such as when our narrator finds himself caught making love with the seductive young woman and struggles throughout the narrative to reconcile his lust/love and his religious views and lifestyle. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Yet in another moment he creates sound arguments for religion and the fact that he proposes that the world consists of signs guiding us towards truth is an argument that can be used to defend belief in the existence of God. Perhaps it is safer to assume that Eco attempts to argue that an over adherence to legalistic religion is dangerous but that at the same time the underlying beliefs of various religions are not necessarily false. <br><br><strong>
<u>Motifs and ideas</u>
</strong><br><br><strong>Corruption and desire</strong><br><br>Umberto Eco’s novel on its surface tackles head on the idea that corruption and desire exists within even the most pure of all men. Each of the monks are described in meticulous detail as men with slight blemishes to their character whether that be the sexual sins of ‘sodomy’ and ‘adultery’ or the other sins of pride and selfish ambition. Eco suggests that all men are at their nature flawed, regardless of how pure an individual is. Yet it seems that some men are more corrupted than others, or rather, some men give into their corruption, using religion as a powerful cover up. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ It is this form of corruption that ultimately destroys the monastery through fire. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br><strong>Visions and herbs</strong><br><br><em>The Name of the Rose</em> contains several passages of fantastic visions, which the narrator describes in detail.  These visions link-up to the mystery of the novel and help link back to the religious content of the book (which itself refers often to the <em>Book of Revelations</em>). At the same time, these dream sequences create a sense of the narrator being unreliable, though throughout most of the text he appears entirely reliable. This sense of unreliability is compounded by the reference to various hallucinogenic herbs, which are utilised in the monastery or eaten/burned by the monks. As a final note, it is particularly interesting that like Sherlock Holmes the protagonist appears to be addicted to certain herbs, thus perpetuating the idea that all good detectives must rely on performance enhancing drugs.  <br><br><strong>Fire</strong><br><br>There is the sense in this novel about the dual nature of fire, much as Eco seems to be conflicted in his views on religion. Fire is a recurring motif in this novel and is used to represent emotional desire and the ability to purify. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Particularly in the sequences where the narrator describes his making love to the young woman and the burning of the heretic. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br><strong>
<u> Conclusion</u>
</strong><br><br><em>The Name of the Rose</em> is a fine work, a beautiful work and yet a convoluted and bizarre work of fiction. It is perhaps one of the deeper works of fiction in any genre, willing to ask big questions without resolving them and packaging such thoughts within a historical mystery. Perhaps one of the best links to reality that this novel creates is the reflection that reality is a mystery to those within it and yet when outside of that reality one can observe the mystery’s secret. <br><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="4773809990195945295" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>
</div>
<div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
<tr class="no_border" id="update_comment_stuff_Review435886426">

<span id="freeTextContainer4575055481565124351">I only just discovered these comments. <br><br>My initial point is that when I really enjoy a book, one of my favourite things to do is to also hunt out the o</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText4575055481565124351" style="display:none">I only just discovered these comments. <br><br>My initial point is that when I really enjoy a book, one of my favourite things to do is to also hunt out the one star or two star reviews and read to find the differing opinions. It really helps me open my eyes to what the issues are with a text according to others. Only here the one star reviews weren't very in depth so there weren't very many detailed opinions to look at more just dismissive 'I don't like it' or 'it was too long/boring for me'. Which is all fine - everyone is entitled to an opinion but I was hoping for more substance to gain insight.</br></br></span>

<div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_176214867" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/176214867" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat" title="Jan-Maat"><img alt="Jan-Maat" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1407579968p2/5617661.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/176214867?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Jun 13, 2014</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat" title="Jan-Maat">Jan-Maat</a>

          added it


          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/611484.The_Name_of_the_Rose" title="Paperback 9780099466031">review of another edition</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=novel">novel</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=read-in-translation">read-in-translation</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=italy">italy</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer176214867" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer837669378089573848">A surprising novel, masquerading as a piece of historical fiction, all very proper inside its fake framing narrative, but also managing to be a spoof murder-mystery.<br><br>The main character is William of Baskerville who has a Watson like side-kick.  He may not use cocaine but he does eat 'certain herbs' and some of his description is lifted from that of a famous resident of Baker Street.  And wait, a isn't a monastery with it's hidden conflicts and desires awfully similar in some ways to a country house</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText837669378089573848" style="display:none">A surprising novel, masquerading as a piece of historical fiction, all very proper inside its fake framing narrative, but also managing to be a spoof murder-mystery.<br><br>The main character is William of Baskerville who has a Watson like side-kick.  He may not use cocaine but he does eat 'certain herbs' and some of his description is lifted from that of a famous resident of Baker Street.  And wait, a isn't a monastery with it's hidden conflicts and desires awfully similar in some ways to a country house?  However in line with Eco's approach the resolution is more sheer luck than sherlock.<br><br>The title suggestively evokes <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/332226.The_Romance_of_the_Rose" title="The Romance of the Rose by Guillaume de Lorris">The Romance of the Rose</a>, although here the beloved flower projected in the walled garden is not an allegorical representation of woman but of learning and the power of knowledge to transform our outlook on the world.  The figure of Jealously literally embodied in the monk who protects this great treasure, not simply with a castle's walls but inside a labyrinth library with a secret reading room.<br><br>The form of the labyrinthine library is taken from Borges <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/172366.The_Library_of_Babel" title="The Library of Babel by Jorge Luis Borges">The Library of Babel</a> and so the story proceeds with layers of references to surprise and amuse, however if you are in danger of floundering among them the trick is to find out what the hidden book at the centre of the library is all about and to keep that in mind for every reread.<br><br>The relationship between truth, myth and invention is a theme that <em>In the Name of the Rose</em> shares with <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22373884.Foucaults_Pendulum" title="Foucaults Pendulum by Umberto Eco">Foucaults Pendulum</a> and <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10507.Baudolino" title="Baudolino by Umberto Eco">Baudolino</a>.  Here we are introduced to the mysterious room and its hidden content which we can take as a metaphor for the meaning of life (or the quest for meaning in life maybe), but with intelligence, guile and luck the heroes can know the truth.  In <em>Foucaults Pendulum</em> by contrast the hidden room is unreachable <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[which given the ending of <em>In the Name of the Rose</em> is unsurprising! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and its potential contents are contested among many groups and individuals  whose need for meaning is so great that they are vulnerable to anyone’s' and everyone’s' claims of knowing what is inside that room even if (with shades of <em>Baudolino</em>) that 'knowledge' is the purist of inventions, unsullied by any relation to reality.<br><br>The problem in Eco's universe is not the hidden room or its contents but in the attitude of people towards it.  In <em>Foucaults Pendulum</em> the Diabolicals take it terribly seriously with fatal consequences, here, its protector fears the content so terribly that no one can remain safe, yet that book seems to me to embody Eco's attitude to all readers of these three books <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ in other words, don't take it too seriously  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br><br>God fun with theology and medieval philosophising and good fun for all readers.<!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="837669378089573848" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>
</div>

<div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_548757569" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/548757569" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7810481" title="طَيْف"><img alt="طَيْف" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1331971829p2/7810481.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/548757569?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Mar 09, 2013</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/7810481" title="طَيْف">طَيْف</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/16082109" title="Paperback 8845207005">review of another edition</a>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer548757569" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer7816524803802029632"><strong>"اسم الوردة"<br><br>حين يكون القاتل كتابا<br><br><br>فشهوة المعرفة أودت بحياة أصحابها...في زمن حرّمت فيه الحقيقة...وطمست غشاوة على أعين الباحثين عنها<br><br>المكان: ديرٌ بندكيتي يثير الرهبة في النفس، يقبع في منعزل في الشمال الإيطالي، ورغم عزلته التامة يبدو متأثرا بالخلافات الفكريّة وحسابات المصالح خارجه بين التيارات المسيحية المختلفة، ومكتبة ضخمة بدهاليز وأحاجي غامضة، يفتخر أهل الدير بما تحويه من كتب يحرم على الكثيرين ما في بطونها من معرفة خوفا من تأثير الحقيقة في تغيير الواقع<br><br><br>الزمان: القرن الرابع عشر، في ظل أجواء الخلافات</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></strong></span>
<span id="freeText7816524803802029632" style="display:none"><strong>"اسم الوردة"<br><br>حين يكون القاتل كتابا<br><br><br>فشهوة المعرفة أودت بحياة أصحابها...في زمن حرّمت فيه الحقيقة...وطمست غشاوة على أعين الباحثين عنها<br><br>المكان: ديرٌ بندكيتي يثير الرهبة في النفس، يقبع في منعزل في الشمال الإيطالي، ورغم عزلته التامة يبدو متأثرا بالخلافات الفكريّة وحسابات المصالح خارجه بين التيارات المسيحية المختلفة، ومكتبة ضخمة بدهاليز وأحاجي غامضة، يفتخر أهل الدير بما تحويه من كتب يحرم على الكثيرين ما في بطونها من معرفة خوفا من تأثير الحقيقة في تغيير الواقع<br><br><br>الزمان: القرن الرابع عشر، في ظل أجواء الخلافات الكنسية والصراعات على مناطق النفوذ التي تعدت حدود المعقول، والتي سادتها أحداث دمويّة تقشعر لها الأبدان<br><br><br>سبعة أيام كانت مدار تلك الأحداث التي رويت على لسان "أدسو"، والذي رافق الراهب"غوليالمو" للتحقيق في جريمة قتل تبعتها عدة جرائم في ذلك الدير...و"غوليالمو" عمل محققا في محاكم التفتيش لمحاربة الهرطقة، ولكنه رجل يتبع المنطق ولذلك شعر بالظلم المترتب على هذا العمل فتركه...ليكلف بحل هذا اللغز في ظل الظروف السائدة...ومن خلال الدلالات والتأويلات والبناء على الافتراضات المنطقية تارة والمصادفات تارة أخرى استطاع اكتشاف الحقيقة والتي تعدت كونها جرائم قتل عاديّة...فـ "يورج" الأعمى الذي يحرِّم الضحك بحجة أن السيد المسيح لم يضحك مطلقا، "يورج" صاحب الفلسفة الغريبة...يرى أن المعرفة ستطيح بالكثير من البنية الفكرية السائدة وستخلق معرفة مضادة لا يريد لها الظهور...فبرأيه عن أرسطو يقول:<br><br><em><br>" كل كتاب لذلك الرجل حطم جزءاً من المعرفة التي جمعتها المسيحية طيلة قرون، إن سفر التكوين أورد ما يجب معرفته عن تركيب الكون وما أن اكتشفت كتب الفيلسوف الفيزيائية حتى أعيد التفكير في الكون بمعنى المادة الصماء اللزجة وحتى كاد العربي ابن رشد أن يقنع الجميع بسرمدية العالم "</br></em><br><br>لذلك يخفي كتابا يعد معرفة ما فيه خطرا في قاعة تسمى "أقصى أفريقيا"، لا يمكن دخولها إلا من ممرات سريّة، ضمن دهاليز مكتبة الدير الملتوية...ليكون سببا في جرائم قتل متعددة<br><br>نعيش تلك الأيام السبعة برفقة "غوليالمو" و"أدسو" ما بين ليل الدير ونهاره...وتناقضهما الذي ينعكس على الحياة فيه...فالليل بظلامه الدامس كفيل بإظهار ما لا يُرى، بل ربما كانت الرؤية فيه أوضح لما يحرص الجميع على إخفائه نهارا من نزوات ونزعات مختفية خلال الرتم اليومي العادي لروتين الحياة في الدير من صلوات ولقاءات وأحاديث وحتى الكتب التي تنسخ وتختفي من بعد في دهاليز مكتبة الدير فلا يظهر بعضها في وضح النهار نهائيا<br><br><br>ربما جذبتني الحبكة البوليسية التي كانت جزءا من أحداث الرواية والتي وضعتني أمام تحدّ اكتشاف القاتل وأسباب القتل ومدى ارتباطها بالأحداث خارج الدير أو الأحداث داخله أو ذلك الكتاب الذي ثارت حوله الشبهات، ولكن ما أمتعني أكثر بعدها الفلسفي الذي جعلني أعيد قراءة بعض الصفحات مرات ومرات للبحث عما وراء تلك الحروف لمحاكمة الماضي وإسقاطها على الحاضر...فالرواية تحتشد بالرموز والإشارات والإمكانات المتعددة لتأويل النص...إنها عالم غني بالمتناقضات والتفاصيل والخيال والمنطق...وقد نجح "إيكو" في استدراجنا من خلال حبكته البوليسية للإغراق برؤاه الفلسفية<br><br><br>أحببت الرواية...رغم أنني أصبت بحالة من الملل خلال العديد من الصفحات التي أغرقت تارة في الفلسفة وتارة في الأحداث الكهنوتية السائدة في تلك الفترة...والذي شوش عليّ متعتي، وكم وددت لو كانت أبسط وأوضح حتى لا تفقد جماليتها.<br><br><br>بالنسبة لي كانت النهاية المكافأة الكبرى لما احتجته من صبر خلال القراءة، فالقاتل كتاب سممت صفحاته، دلالة على ما يعنيه الاقتراب من الحقيقة في ذلك الزمن، والمكتبة بكل ما تحتويه تعرضت للفناء، وربما هذا ما يحتاجه بدء عهد جديد يبنى على الحقائق لا على الأوهام والخرافات...هذا هو الثمن المستحق لتلك الجرائم والتي تعدت جرائم القتل...فالجريمة الكبرى كانت ما وراء تلك الجرائم<br><br>في مشهد الحريق يقول غوليالمو لأدسو:<br><u><br>"يمكن أن يولد الدجال حتى من التقوى...من فرط محبة الله أو الحقيقة كما يتولد الهرطيق من القديس والممسوس من العرّاف...احترس يا "أدسو" من أولئك المستعدين للموت من أجل الحقيقة لأنهم يجرون معهم عادة إلى الموت كثيرين آخرين عوضا عنهم...كان "يورج" يخشى الكتاب الثاني لأرسطو ربما لأنه كان يعلمنا كيف نمسخ وجه كل حقيقة حتى لا نصبح عبيد أوهامنا"<br/></br></u><br><br><br>أعجبني ما أظهرته الرواية بطريقة أو بأخرى حول الإسهامات التي قدمها العرب ووجدت طريقها لمكتبات أوروبا، بل وبني عليها الكثير من علومهم...في وقت كانت تتخبط فيه في ظلمات تعاليم الكنيسة الرافضة لمنطق العقل والعلم".<br><br><br>اسم الوردة: رواية تحمل العديد من التأويلات من اسمها وحتى نهايتها<br> ستعثر أثناء قراءتها على العديد منها وستكتشف أخرى بعد الانتهاء منها، وربما تحتاج قراءة ثانية لاكتشاف المزيد...ولكنها بالتأكيد ستشحن تفكيرك ومخيلتك طويلا<br><br>"كانت الوردة اسما، ونحن لا نمسك إلا الأسماء"<br><br><br>لا أستطيع منحها أقل من خمس نجمات متجاوزة ما أصابني من ملل أحيانا، وإغراق في الفلسفة حد اللافهم أحيانا أخرى.<br/></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></strong></span>
<a data-text-id="7816524803802029632" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_155174342" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/155174342" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2846441-karla" title="Karla"><img alt="Karla" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1413656093p2/2846441.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/155174342?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Oct 23, 2012</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/2846441-karla" title="Karla">Karla</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/379262.The_Name_of_the_Rose" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780446344104">review of another edition</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2846441-karla?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2846441-karla?shelf=cover-artists-sanjulian">cover-artists-sanjulian</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2846441-karla?shelf=medieval">medieval</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2846441-karla?shelf=historical-mystery">historical-mystery</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2846441-karla?shelf=1st-person-pov">1st-person-pov</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2846441-karla?shelf=banging-aint-just-for-the-pulpit">banging-aint-just-for-the-pulpit</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2846441-karla?shelf=book-to-film">book-to-film</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer155174342" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer2658106627726022928">A lot has already been said about this for me to rehash the basics or try to address the themes (and my pathetic attempts to sound SMRT would be embarrassing), so I'll simply recount the feels I had.<br><br>* All that theological debating? More fascinating than I'd ever thought possible.<br><br>* Medieval church history! OMG, you medieval guys, God's representative on Earth was a corrupt motherfucker. But I guess the Holy Hot Pincers of Chastisement kept you all nodding your heads and staying in line (more or l</br></br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText2658106627726022928" style="display:none">A lot has already been said about this for me to rehash the basics or try to address the themes (and my pathetic attempts to sound SMRT would be embarrassing), so I'll simply recount the feels I had.<br><br>* All that theological debating? More fascinating than I'd ever thought possible.<br><br>* Medieval church history! OMG, you medieval guys, God's representative on Earth was a corrupt motherfucker. But I guess the Holy Hot Pincers of Chastisement kept you all nodding your heads and staying in line (more or less).<br><br>* The huge amount of splinter sects and "heretical" offshoots are mind-boggling, and they're all convinced the others are wrong because The Popular Front thinks Christ only farted on Sundays and the People's Front insists it was Thursday afternoons after the tea &amp; cucumber sammiches or some such crap. No wonder the Middle Ages were a mess (and continuing a long tradition of dogmatic asshattery).<br><br><img class="escapedImg" height="225" src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380347143/701509.jpg" width="400"><br><em>Follow the gourd! Following the sandal is just silly.</em><br><br>* While all the semiotic stuff whizzed right over my head, I was still glued to the pages and didn't skip a word and it didn't feel like a chore. *pats self on back*<br><br>* Cool! The last part of the book was slightly different than the movie! Even so, the fact that the mystery plot itself adhered closely to the book didn't make it boring or a feeling of arrgh-I've-seen-this-already. On the contrary, there was so much new stuff with the history and theology that the whole thing was super-fresh and exciting.<br><br>* The humor. I loved Adso's narration and the coming-of-age aspect of it, as well as his older-self-looking-back-on-his-younger-self perspective. He and William had a great rapport (another thing that the movie nailed).<br><br>* That abbey was one gay place.<br><br>As is obvious, I'd seen the movie before reading the book, and I think it made the reading experience a bit easier so I could focus on the theological debates, the history, and Eco's narrative style. I'd recommend seeing the movie first, especially if you're approaching the book as a random read and not as a literary scholar (which I ain't, as is obvious).<br><br>I greatly enjoyed it and am glad that I finally sacked up and read it after being intimidated by it for so long.<br><br><strong>ETA:</strong> Whoops...Rosemary Rogers. I mentioned that in my last update. OK, so, this book....awesome and page-turning! I understand what's going on and being said as I read, but just TRY to tell someone else what's going on and what's being said? Pffft. Forget it. Read it for yourself. That's been the exact same experience I've had with every Rosemary Rogers book I've ever read.<br><br>If these two ever collaborated on a book together, my head would esplode.</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></img></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="2658106627726022928" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="review" id="review_26941407" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26941407" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1262683-terry" title="Terry "><img alt="Terry " src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1316628758p2/1262683.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/26941407?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Oct 18, 2013</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/1262683-terry" title="Terry ">Terry </a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/335061.The_Name_of_the_Rose" title="Paperback 9780330284141">review of another edition</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1262683-terry?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1262683-terry?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer26941407" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer16644595572375630620">This is one of my 're-read often' books. The story of Adso and his mentor Brother William as they encounter the nefarious secrets of the abbey they have journeyed to somewhere in the heart of Italy is fantastic. Brother William is the unflappable, Sherlock-like investigator first asked by the Abbot to look into some mysterious deaths and then told to stop when he gets too near the truth. Driven by his hunger for knowledge, William attempts to see beyond the rumours of apocalypse and the presence</span>
<span id="freeText16644595572375630620" style="display:none">This is one of my 're-read often' books. The story of Adso and his mentor Brother William as they encounter the nefarious secrets of the abbey they have journeyed to somewhere in the heart of Italy is fantastic. Brother William is the unflappable, Sherlock-like investigator first asked by the Abbot to look into some mysterious deaths and then told to stop when he gets too near the truth. Driven by his hunger for knowledge, William attempts to see beyond the rumours of apocalypse and the presence of the devil to find the true human evil at the heart of the mystery.<br><br>Following William is his innocent companion, the novice Adso, who tells us this tale of his youth from the perspective of old age. In this tour-de-force by Eco we come across a motley crew of strange characters from the pitable and beastly Salvatore, and the urbane and pompous Abbot Abo to the driven zealot Bernardo Gui (an actualy historical figure painted in somewhat extreme colours by Eco). Woven into the mystery plot and panoply of characters is a dazzling mix of medieval politics and philosophy as the abbey is playing host to a meeting between rival theological factions: the Franciscans who hope to validate their beliefes and way of life in the midst of controversy and their enemies who hope to squash this rival group in its infancy.<br><br>To me, reading Eco has been an experience of diminishing returns, as each novel he has written seems to have fallen further and further from the heights achieved by this novel until I could not even finish _Baudolino_ due to its unreadability. That being said _The Name of the Rose_ is a classic and I highly encourage any and all to enter into its fascinating world.</br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="16644595572375630620" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<span id="freeText10060345546828801154" style="display:none">I've read several of his and agree that <em>The Name of The Rose</em> is the best and most deserving of a re-read (I'm up to 2). <div class="section">
<div class="review" id="review_100256636" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/100256636" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2088163-kevin-neilson" title="Kevin Neilson"><img alt="Kevin Neilson" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1242063463p2/2088163.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/100256636?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Apr 27, 2010</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/2088163-kevin-neilson" title="Kevin Neilson">Kevin Neilson</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer100256636" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer17990989749535090818">What a didactic, tedious, prolix piece of trash!  Eco writes whole paragraphs in Latin and then leaves them untranslated, because he's such an awesome polyglot that chicks want to do him.  Readers are also expected to know Dutch.  Eco likes to hear himself talk, too.  Want to hear pedantic 14th-century theological arguments that stretch on for pages and have nothing to do with the plot?  You've got it!  Want a lame Dan Brown mystery, with the same stilted dialogue, but embellished with entire chapters</span>
<span id="freeText17990989749535090818" style="display:none">What a didactic, tedious, prolix piece of trash!  Eco writes whole paragraphs in Latin and then leaves them untranslated, because he's such an awesome polyglot that chicks want to do him.  Readers are also expected to know Dutch.  Eco likes to hear himself talk, too.  Want to hear pedantic 14th-century theological arguments that stretch on for pages and have nothing to do with the plot?  You've got it!  Want a lame Dan Brown mystery, with the same stilted dialogue, but embellished with entire chapters of the author showing off how much trivia he knows about ancient Arab codices?  No problem!  The guy is such a tool that this is how he describes himself on the jacket:  <em>Umberto Eco is a world-famous specialist in semiotics, a distinguished historian, philosopher, aesthetician, and scholar whose interests range from St. Thomas Aquinas to James Joyce to Superman.</em>  I'm going to hurl.  Aesthetician?  Please.  More like <em>ass</em>thetician.<br><br>Do you think I'm exaggerating?  I've proof:  I've copied this terrible passage wherein the hero discovers how to enter the secret chamber, using his knowledge of the genitive case in Latin.  Be careful not to hurl as you read this. <br><br><blockquote>[<em>The old Monk, William, and the narrator apprentice, Adso, are hiding out in the stables.</em>:]<br>To his right, the the third animal in line raised his head, sensing our presence, and whinnied.  I smiled.  "Tertius equi," I said.<br>    "What?" William asked.<br>    "Nothing.  I was remembering poor Salvatore.  He wanted to perform God knows what magic with that horse, and, with his fractured Latin he called him 'tertius equi.'  Which would be the u."<br>    "The u?" asked William, who had heard my prattle without paying much attention to it.<br>    "Yes, because 'tertiu equi' does not mean the third horse, but the third of the horse, and the third letter of the word 'equus' is u.  But this is all nonsense..."<br>    William looked at me, and in the darkness I seemed to see his face transformed.  "God bless you, Adso!" he said to me.  "Why, of course, suppositio materialis, the discourse is presumed de dicto and not de re....What a fool I am!"  He gave himself such a great blow on the forehead that I heard a clap, and I believe he hurt himself.  "My boy, this is the second time today that wisdom has spoken through your mouth, first in dream and now in waking!  Run, run to your cell and fetch the lamp, or, rather, both lamps we hid.  Let no one see you, and join me in the church at once!  Ask no questions."<br>...<br>    I ran into the church.  William was under the tripod and was rereading the parchment with Venantius's notes.<br>    "Adso," he said to me, "'primum et septimum de equator' does not mean the first and seventh of four, but of the four, the word 'four'!"  For a moment I still did not understand, but then I was enlightened:  "Super thronos viginti quatuor!  The writing!  The verse!  The words are carved over the mirror!"<br>    "Come," William said, "perhaps we are still in time to save a life!"<br>
</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></blockquote></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="17990989749535090818" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>

<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer551419742" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer9252757437782678083">يا حسافة على الوقت الذي ضاع في قراءة هذه الرواية <br>من عيوبها أنها <br>طويلة جدا <br>مملة <br>اسماء وشخصيات كثيرة <br>تفاصيل دقيقة وكثيرة بشكل مبالغ فيه <br>ومن أعظم عيوبها بالنسبة لي تشبيهها برواية عزازييل والزعم بأن عزازييل تعتبر للمبتدئين مقارنة بهذه الرواية <br>ما أقول الا رحم الله" كفار العرب-على وصف المؤلف" على ابدعاتهم الغابرة والحاضرة <br>مثلا في رواية عزازييل كان المفصل في الاختلاف على التوحيد والثالوثية أما في هذه الرواية عن فقر المسيح عليه السلام! <br><br>وأخيرا وليس آخرا <br>الترجمة الرديئة للرواية اللي ذل أهلنا فيها المترجم</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>

<div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_62185754" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/62185754" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely" title="K.D. Absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1420066396p2/2156200.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/62185754?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Jan 04, 2015</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely" title="K.D. Absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/611484.The_Name_of_the_Rose" title="Paperback 9780099466031">review of another edition</a>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer62185754'); return false;">click here.</a>
</em>
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer62185754" style="display:none">
<span id="freeTextContainer9535543789747781142">I spent 1 full week reading this book. Not only because it has almost 500 dense pages but also because I suffered a terrible backache while reading this. So I had to turn and change my sitting or lying position every few minutes while reading.<br><br>The story revolves around William of Baskervilles and inquisitor and Adso a novice monk. They were ask to investigate the killing that happened in a rich Italian abbey during the medieval period (1327). It is a mix of whodunnit (like the recent novel I read</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText9535543789747781142" style="display:none">I spent 1 full week reading this book. Not only because it has almost 500 dense pages but also because I suffered a terrible backache while reading this. So I had to turn and change my sitting or lying position every few minutes while reading.<br><br>The story revolves around William of Baskervilles and inquisitor and Adso a novice monk. They were ask to investigate the killing that happened in a rich Italian abbey during the medieval period (1327). It is a mix of whodunnit (like the recent novel I read of Agatha Christie, THE MURDER OF ROGER ACKROYD) and historical novel set in medieval period (like Ken Follett's PILLARS OF THE EARTH). Naturally, I started leafing the pages with gusto because I liked both novels.<br><br>However, there are just too many details and Latin phrases in the book that can annoy readers who are not fully familiar with that period of European history and that dying language. Also, after the pattern seemingly being followed by the "killer" was later revealed to be a series of coincidences only, the magic of being a mystery novel just vanished. It is as if the climax was built for nothing. With me spending 400+ pages of understanding the details of the pattern was a bit disappointing.<br><br>The narrative is beautiful though. Considering that this was just translated from the original language it was written, Italian is an achievement for the translator. The reason is that the English prose retained its cadence and appropriateness to its ancient Italian setting. It has that feeling of being a classic despite the novel having been written by Umberto Eco only in the early 80's.<br><br>Lately, I have been reading novels written several decades ago or with settings way back many centuries ago and sometimes this makes me feel ancient too. I should read something very contemporary next to this. Otherwise, my backpain might decide to stay as my brain thinks that I am already a senior citizen and having body aches is just but natural. </br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="9535543789747781142" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey" title="Aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1419544181p2/4119460.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/143356950?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Nov 19, 2014</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey" title="Aubrey">Aubrey</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/379262.The_Name_of_the_Rose" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780446344104">review of another edition</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1001-06-12">1001-06-12</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=4-star">4-star</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1000-guardian">1000-guardian</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=italian">italian</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1001-self">1001-self</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=translated-dp">translated-dp</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2012">r-2012</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads">r-goodreads</a>
</div>

<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer143356950" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer1273164971968151323">I have never been so undecided as to what to rate a book. The oscillation from three to four stars and back again was dizzying, and made worse by the fact that I felt that a 3.5 would be a cop out concerning this particular novel. <br><br>The writing was a mix of excellent logical processes and long trains of theological meanderings. I'd find the words blurring before my eyes when the author kept up his lists for too long, or when one of the characters was especially verbose on religious concerns. At le</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText1273164971968151323" style="display:none">I have never been so undecided as to what to rate a book. The oscillation from three to four stars and back again was dizzying, and made worse by the fact that I felt that a 3.5 would be a cop out concerning this particular novel. <br><br>The writing was a mix of excellent logical processes and long trains of theological meanderings. I'd find the words blurring before my eyes when the author kept up his lists for too long, or when one of the characters was especially verbose on religious concerns. At least I have some familiarity with the Bible and its related texts, or I would have been completely lost.<br><br> It was odd, though. It almost seemed that the author planned it that way, as detective monk William of Baskerville would often give insights into how ridiculous the whole concept of religion is. This ridiculousness was very apparent in the debates over laughter, heresy, and the differences between the countless denominations. So perhaps it was intended. Made for some very boring passages, I will admit. But the brilliant flashes of common sense and amazing trains of thought were enough to tide me over. I only wish more of the book was like that.<br><br>But I must say, blatant inspiration is blatant. It's been a while since I've read the Sherlock Holmes books, so I'll be modeling my observations after my more recent watching of the BBC series (unorthodox, I know, but it illustrates my point). The overwhelming powers of observation and subsequent deduction, the use of drugs to ameliorate thinking, the meditative states that heightened these neurological processes to a sharp focal point, and especially the final relationship between William and the revealed villain. That heady mix of moral antagonism and intellectual respect was very, very familiar. <br><br>It's a shame that it didn't last, the intelligent state of mind at any rate. William's novice had flashes of insight only when in the presence of his mentor, and the manuscript he writes shows how far back he has slipped into the comfort of religious ignorance. He saw the sense of writing down what happened, at any rate. And his discomfort with his state of mind in view of his memories shows that he isn't completely resigned to the sheep state of blind faith. <br><br>But that doesn't really matter. What matters is that I have a couple more books by this author to get through, and the reading should be interesting if they are as filled with convoluted and contrasting passages as this one.</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="1273164971968151323" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="review" id="review_180913221" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/180913221" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3642045-sue" title="Sue"><img alt="Sue" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1274829293p2/3642045.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/180913221?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Aug 13, 2014</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/3642045-sue" title="Sue">Sue</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/8694005-the-name-of-the-rose" title="Kindle Edition ">review of another edition</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=italy">italy</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=read-2014">read-2014</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=medieval">medieval</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer180913221" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer12494815528937633794">The question becomes-how to review a book that has been read and reviewed by so many, a book that is so well known. Short and sweet I think!<br><br>The basic story is both simple and complex (well there goes my above-stated aim). Brother William of Baskerville has been sent to an Italian abbey of some renown on what is an important and urgent mission, to investigate the possibility of heresy present in that Franciscan setting. William formerly served in the "forces" of the Inquisition but chose to leave</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText12494815528937633794" style="display:none">The question becomes-how to review a book that has been read and reviewed by so many, a book that is so well known. Short and sweet I think!<br><br>The basic story is both simple and complex (well there goes my above-stated aim). Brother William of Baskerville has been sent to an Italian abbey of some renown on what is an important and urgent mission, to investigate the possibility of heresy present in that Franciscan setting. William formerly served in the "forces" of the Inquisition but chose to leave that "august" body lacking their rabid style and beliefs. On arriving at the Abbey, he finds that he has an added problem to solve, mysterious death.<br><br>William's character is the delight of this Medieval novel. He thinks; he questions; he reasons; he believes and has faith; but he also has doubts and uses science and his brain. He is a pre-Renaissance man. With William on his trek is a novice, Adso, the narrator of the story, putting pen to paper later in life. He is William's sounding board and sometime-accomplice.<br><br>So much happens that is of the mind here, and Eco is indeed brilliant at (re)creating ecclesiastical arguments for and against laughter, poverty (and degrees of poverty) and Christ's views on poverty(!). At first I was intimidated but gradually I realized what these arguments were about. Power...the oldest source of argument in the world. I was interested to see the familiar name of Aristotle raised after reading the unfamiliar names of the many heretics. And then there are the murders...what purpose could there be for them. Says William:<br><br><br><em>"In that face, deformed by hatred of philosophy, I saw<br>     for the first time the portrait of the Antichrist....The<br>     Antichrist can be born from piety itself, from excessive<br>     love of God or of the truth, as the heretic is born from<br>     the saint and the possessed from the seer. Fear prophets,<br>     Adso, and those prepared to die for the truth, for as a <br>     rule they make many others die with them, often before <br>     them, at times instead of them."</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></em>  (loc 7983)<br><br><br>Brother William and Umberto Eco could be speaking today.</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="12494815528937633794" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="section">
<div class="review" id="review_100843575" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/100843575" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2569242-kathrina" title="Kathrina"><img alt="Kathrina" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1336653486p2/2569242.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/100843575?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Feb 13, 2011</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/2569242-kathrina" title="Kathrina">Kathrina</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/10489.The_Name_of_the_Rose" title="Paperback 9780446322188">review of another edition</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2569242-kathrina?shelf=great-literary-fiction">great-literary-fiction</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2569242-kathrina?shelf=horror-thriller">horror-thriller</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2569242-kathrina?shelf=italian">italian</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2569242-kathrina?shelf=nyrb-intro-authors">nyrb-intro-authors</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer100843575" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer4423104902254138594">This book is so self-aware, I feel like it's reading over my shoulder right now. I recall, about half-way through, lamenting the fact that my edition contained no footnotes for translating the frequent Latin, Italian, and Greek texts thrown into this narrative. And now, after reaching Eco's last line (in Latin!) I hear him laughing at me. Don't you get it, reader?! Roger Bacon chides the scholar who doesn't make languages his first priority, and as I can only piece together a few Latin words and</span>
<span id="freeText4423104902254138594" style="display:none">This book is so self-aware, I feel like it's reading over my shoulder right now. I recall, about half-way through, lamenting the fact that my edition contained no footnotes for translating the frequent Latin, Italian, and Greek texts thrown into this narrative. And now, after reaching Eco's last line (in Latin!) I hear him laughing at me. Don't you get it, reader?! Roger Bacon chides the scholar who doesn't make languages his first priority, and as I can only piece together a few Latin words and stretch meaning from the context, I come to my own conclusions based on what I have read and learned in the past, as William solves his case the wrong way, but with the right answers. I don't read Latin as Adso doesn't read Greek, as William doesn't read Arabic, as mortal man can never read God's language, and so we make our own assumptions based on what we do know. The world's knowledge is constantly fragmented, and we keep what we can use, we forget or reinvent. One day the library will burn, the database will fail, and the only books that will matter are the ones we've read. And who can read what books? Here I begin my <br><br>            Ode to the Joys of Reading Two Books Simultaneously<br><br>I picked up this book <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5189.Reading_With_Oprah__The_Book_Club_That_Changed_America" title="Reading With Oprah: The Book Club That Changed America by Kathleen Rooney">Reading With Oprah: The Book Club That Changed America</a>, not because I am necessarily interested in the particular books that comprise Oprah's list, but because the phenomenon, as a bookseller, intrigues me. My own attitude as book snob was outed when Cormac McCarthy joined her ranks, and I felt the mass public didn't deserve his offering. You have to earn an appreciation for McCarthy. What the hell do I mean by that? Here, watch me struggle with my conscience: If you tell me your favorite author is Picoult, my recommendation will never be McCarthy, even though I feel he'll always be a more satisfying read. But you're not ready. You don't want to reorganize the furniture in your head, you just want a touch of color on your sofa. And that's ok. Moving furniture is a lot of hard work, and I like an easy read between moves. But my favorite reason to read is to change; that's not everyone's reason. What does this have to do with Name of the Rose, you ask?! Because there was this monk, 600 years ago, running Oprah's book club, and he didn't want <s>McCarthy</s>, I mean, Aristotle, distributed to the masses, because they weren't going to read it right, even if it is in Greek. The book had the ability to cause change. This monk, this librarian, didn't want to cause change, to innovate, he wanted to preserve, to archive. Oprah says she wants change, but by promoting her book list, she's preserving at the same time, labeling titles to the Oprah cult, garnering huge sales for her authors. And now she's quit. What may have been future Oprah picks are hidden in the finis Africae, left only for the chosen to find. My point? I dunno. But Eco tells us that all books are references to other books, and now <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/93269.Earth_Abides" title="Earth Abides by George R. Stewart">Earth Abides</a>speaks to me about the end of reading, preserving, and <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6419887.The_Boy_Who_Harnessed_the_Wind__Creating_Currents_of_Electricity_and_Hope" title="The Boy Who Harnessed the Wind: Creating Currents of Electricity and Hope by William Kamkwamba">The Boy Who Harnessed the Wind: Creating Currents of Electricity and Hope</a> speaks to me about the salvation of reading, innovation, and every damn thing I read reminds me why I read and why the book I haven't read yet is going to make the book I just read even better. I'm filling in the context with my own collection of fragments, and the story is told and told again, and when I'm ready I'm going to move the sofa.</br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="4423104902254138594" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="section">
<div class="review" id="review_550251856" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/550251856" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5396526-amal" title="amal"><img alt="amal" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1397433283p2/5396526.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/550251856?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Mar 09, 2013</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/5396526-amal" title="amal">amal</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/16082109" title="Paperback 8845207005">review of another edition</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5396526-amal?shelf=in-group">in-group</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer550251856" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer863780740399648769">اسم الوردة هي متاهة ايكو الخالية من الورود ، تسجل اعتراضك منذ البداية على استرسال الكاتب وكلماته المتدفقة بكرم حاتمي، لدرجة انك لن تجد موضوع هامشي، كل المواضيع التي ذكرت أخذت نصيبها الوافي من الشرح والتفصيل، ومع ذلك تكمل القراءة لجرعات من الفضول والمتعة يتركها لنا ايكو بين الصفحات. <br><br>رحلة ادسو وغوليالمو إلى الدير تكشف خبايا الدير، الدير الذي يكون ليله مناقض لمايجري من طهر في نهاره، صراعات ومطامع لها واجهة الدين وهو منها براء.<br><br>تتحدث الرواية عن قصص الصراعات الدينية بين الطوائف المسيحية المختلفة، عن ا</br></br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText863780740399648769" style="display:none">اسم الوردة هي متاهة ايكو الخالية من الورود ، تسجل اعتراضك منذ البداية على استرسال الكاتب وكلماته المتدفقة بكرم حاتمي، لدرجة انك لن تجد موضوع هامشي، كل المواضيع التي ذكرت أخذت نصيبها الوافي من الشرح والتفصيل، ومع ذلك تكمل القراءة لجرعات من الفضول والمتعة يتركها لنا ايكو بين الصفحات. <br><br>رحلة ادسو وغوليالمو إلى الدير تكشف خبايا الدير، الدير الذي يكون ليله مناقض لمايجري من طهر في نهاره، صراعات ومطامع لها واجهة الدين وهو منها براء.<br><br>تتحدث الرواية عن قصص الصراعات الدينية بين الطوائف المسيحية المختلفة، عن اقصاء المخالف وإتهامه بالهرطقة وإلصاق الشائعات عن ممارسات إباحية ووحشية مثل قربان الطفل الرضيع الذي يقطع ويخلط بالدقيق، بعض هذه التهم عمرها عشرات السنين استخدمت من قبل بين طوائف سابقة ويعاد استخدامها كلما دعت الحاجة والإختلاف.<br><br>التشابه في الأحداث مع طوائف وأديان أخرى مذهل، الصراعات والحجج ذاتها، لأن طبيعتها الحقيقة ترتبط بطبيعة الإنسان متغيرة، تُظهر الدين كلباس فضفاض يتغير باستمرار ليلائم الأشخاص وأطماعهم الدنيوية.<br><br>الجرائم تتوالى في سبعة أيام قضاها غوليالمو في الدير، يحاول كشف المستور في وسط من المحظورات والكثير من الوعظ، تطلّ الحقيقة برأسها الجليدي بين التفاصيل الصغيرة، تنصدم من السبب وسذاجة الكتاب الضاحك لأرسطو ؛ لكنها صورة عن الحقيقة، كثير من الحروب والصراعات تقوم على فكرة ساذجة أو أناس سذّج، مثل خدم الدير الذين يسيرون على غير هدى مع النعاج التائهة وقت إحتراق الدير.<br><br>العلماء العرب وسيرتهم العطرة (العلماء الكفار) كما أسماهم الرهبان، يذكر ايكو الكثير عن كتبهم مثل النباتي والخازن والكندي والخوارزمي البلداشي وابن رشد، عن مكتبات بغداد الست والثلاثين، والعشرة الآف مخطوط لابن العلقمي، ومكتبة طرابلس التي تحوي ستة ملايين كتاب، الألفين وأربعمائة مصحف في القاهرة، تاريخ غنيّ نقرأ عنه في ثنايا رواية شيء جميل ويضيف قيمة للرواية. <br><br>يقرأ ادسو العاشق في تعاريف الحب عند العرب :<br>ابن حزم : هو مرض عضال دواؤه فيه والمريض لايريد الشفاء منه <br>ابن سينا : هو هاجس معذب ذو طبيعة كئيبة، ينشأ من التفكير وإعادة التفكير في قسمات وحركات أو عادات شخص من جنس مقابل، لاينشأ كمرض ولكنه يصبح مرضا عندما لا يلاقي ارضاء فيصبح هاجسا استحواذيا.<br><br>نعود للصراعات الدينية وأذكر بعض ماورد من مبررات في المحاكمة :<br>- ربما كنا فريسة رغبة مفرطة في العدالة، يمكن ارتكاب الخطيئة من الإفراط في حب الرب<br>- لماذا تعيبون علينا اراقة قليل من الدماء إن كانت من أجل إعادة العدالة إلى نصابها <br><br>رئيس الدير يحذر غوليالمو من كشف فساد الرهبان :<br>عندما يخطئ الراعي ينبغي إبعاده عن الرعاة، ولكن الويل إذا ماأخذت النعاج ترتاب من الرعاة ! <br><br>يورج يخاف على الكلمة الألهية من هرطقة العلماء، من ضحك أرسطو ومن سرمدية ابن رشد ويعممّ خوفه وفكره هذا على البقية بدون خيار منهم بل وحتى بدون علمهم !  <br>يقول عنه غوليالمو : الشيطان ليس أمير المادة، الشيطان هو صلف الفكر، هو الإيمان دون إبتسام.<br><br>رواية ممتعة فيها الكثير من الملل والتشويق تستحق القراءة وبشكل متواصل لأن التجزئة ستُفقدها الكثير. <br><br><a href="http://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/1232215-3-2013?format=html&amp;page=1">هنا</a> قراءتنا للرواية في صالون الجمعة</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="863780740399648769" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>
